```
In article <78jjvaF1mguhsU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article <78gm17F1lt2gbU1@mid.individual.net>,
>> Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>>> docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

[snip]

>>>> Mr Dashwood, it might do one well to recall that the value and
>>>> nature of opinions, in and of themselvelves, remains, as has been
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>Yes. Thank you.

You're quite welcome.  My keyboard gets a bit sloppy at times, perhaps it 
is time to bring it in for re-grouting and de-lousing.

[snip]

>Given a choice between a Drill instructor and a Manager, as giving advice to 
>follow, I would go with the Drill Instructor. While it is a limited subset 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>advantageous.

Nothing is absolute, Mr Dashwood, including this statement.  Based on my 
experience - limited as it may be - I've concluded the following: most of 
the Drill Sergeants I've known (including the one I cite) take it as a 
mark of pride and honor that they are able to mold a disparate group of 
teen-aged boys (yes, it *was* that far back) into a group that would March 
Into Hell for Them.  Period.  There's a machine-gun, throwing live fire, 
downhill at your squadron... and the Sergeant says 'On my order, take 
'em'!'... and the kids, for the most part do.  Period.

The relationship is also reciprocal.  Somebody screws with the troops and 
the Sergeant marches into an office - it doesn't matter whose office - and 
after a brief formulaic exchange of 'Request permission to speak my mind, 
Sir' and a 'Permission Granted' the Sergeant bellows the equivalent of 
'WHO DO YOU THINK YOU ARE TO TREAT *MY* MEN THIS WAY?!?'

As I've also admitted before I'm a consultant/contractor/hired gun and I 
see the insides of Sick Shops.  In those the Managers pride themselves, 
among each other, on getting as much as they can from their subordinates 
while returning as little as possible in the opposite direction.

(Yes, I know, *you* have done things differently... you ain't everybody)

Then... when it comes time for a subordinate to request Managerial support 
the result is usually - again, in my experience - a shoulder-shrug and a 
'Sorry, nothing I can do, it just works out that way, You Know How It 
Goes.'

Based on these experiences I concluded that not only would I not be a Good 
Manager... but given a choice I'll take the Drill Sergeant model *and* 
results any day of the week.  I want my men to March Into Hell For Me... 
and I want them to know that I'll storm into a General's Office on their 
behalf, as well.

[snip]

>> The world is not run by trash-collectors, either, Mr Dashwood, and yet
>> they seem, at times, to be able to... pick up something of value.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>reference?

One that might have been a bit obscure... 'blessed are those who find 
treasures among pebbles, they are always surrounded by riches.'

[snip]

>> but making accusations of insobriety is *so* much easier,
>> for some, than indulging in the discourse which begins with 'I am not
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>Now where's the fun in that, Doc? :-)

Possibly in the same place as the fun in getting something of learning and 
use, Mr Dashwood.

DD
```

```
It could be that you won't ever get too tired or hurt to retire, or
that you won't have the means to retire, or that there is nothing that
you like spending your time programming for other people.

Or maybe, it is better to enjoy what we're doing sufficiently until we
have better alternatives.   (As long as we prepare to have those
alternatives and a choice when times change - or when we change).
```

```
the futility of the language du jour approach older but eminently more
useful languages will see their resurgence in serious programming.
```

```
Which professionals are you talking about?   The ones in Human

the new IS system?   The ones who learn their language at home with
the cheapest available tools?   The university professors and their
bosses?   The ones looking for jobs?   

It is not obvious to me who will have both the desire and the power to
make this change.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <78a19bF1ludk0U1@mid.individual.net> <I0VTl.5862$Gx.1307@newsfe04.iad> <78erb7F1lnmgaU1@mid.individual.net> <2op725d7a40b9fp238l1aj244in9ff7o69@4ax.com> <78i9fiF1lqt4kU2@mid.individual.net> <r608259rahbv19k6i6nc33018g9q2lhfnp@4ax.com> <78if26F1md54fU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <r608259rahbv19k6i6nc33018g9q2lhfnp@4ax.com>,
> Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> writes:
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> programming.

Are you serious?

You'll see steamships and triremes replacing container ships, bronze swords 
and flint arrowheads replacing high velocity rifles, trebuchets and 
catapults replacing interballistic nuclear missiles, and you'll sit down to 
a meal of dodo pie and mastodon steaks, before you see the world turn "back" 
to COBOL.

What you dismiss as the "languages du jour" are NOT fashion statements at 
all. They have changed the world of IT and are continuing to do so. These 
languages (and approaches, like OOP) arose in response to a rapidly evolving 
hardware architecture. The hardware changed and the software HAD to change 
to keep abreast of it. Sadly, (for some), that impact has had far-reaching 
consequences and shown up your "eminently more useful" (for what, exactly? 
Show me something you can do with one of these "eminently more useful" 
languages that I CAN'T do with my choice of "language du jour"...) languages 
to have the feet of clay they have always had, when it comes to meeting 
today's requirements. ONLY when the requrement is the same as yesteryear, 
does it make sense to use the "old" approaches. And that is the nub of it. 
Some people simply can't see that the requirements today are "different", 
some people don't WANT the requirement to be different, and some people 
don't WANT ANYTHING to be different... ever.
(And there are also a sprinkling of cases where the nature of the busness is 
such that it really HASN'T changed markedly, and the old approach is 
perfectly OK. These businesses are in an ever-diminishing minority...)

How can we reasonably expect a language that was designed for a single 
processing thread on a centralised architecture with full control of all 
peripheral operations by the program, to be equally at home in a 
distributed, multi-threading, multiprocessing environment, which is largely 
event driven? You might as well blame a seagull for not being a kestrel.

I don't blame COBOL for being unfit for 2009; I know what it was designed 
for... (I was there... :-) well, almost...:-)) It served long and well for 
much longer than anyone could have foreseen.

To equate "(real) professionals" with people who DON'T know modern languages 
is not only inaccurate, it is offensive to people who do take computer 
programming seriously. (That would be me...most of the time...)

I shudder to think what you had in mind when you wrote "serious 
programming"... so I won't go there.

Pete.
```

```
Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

[snip]

>GM's profit is predicated in selling more cars than people want to
>buy.

I disagree, Mr Brazee.  Leaving aside The Economist's dictum of 'cash is 
fact, profit is opinion'... if GM makes enough money on the interest it 
collects on the loans made via one of its finanacial subsidiaries then 
GM's profit is predicated on lending money to qualified borrowers.

(Olde Joke #723: Upon reaching a certain level of management at BigCorp a 
new manager is called into the Corner Office.  After sinking into the 
large, soft leather chair the Corner Office Manager - this is so very old 
a joke that folks in Corner Offices actually *managed*, they were not 
Idiots - casts a steely eye upon the newbie and says 'So tell me, Jones... 
what does BigCorp *make*?'

No matter what Jones responds - and I've heard this joke told about many 
industries, autos, glassware, rubber goods, agricultural products - the 
Manager snaps back with a simple '*Wrong*!  BigCorp makes... money.')

Remember: insurance companies do not make most of their money on the 
premium-to-payment ratios of their policies, they make most of their money 
on the interest they reap from investing their premiums solidly.

DD
```

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sun, 31 May 2009 19:53:10 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

The argument for getting rid of COBOL began before I became a COBOL 
programmer in 1973, and then, as now, I have yet to hear a valid reason 
'for' the argument.  The only reason I can see that some want to get rid 
of COBOL is the same argument that each generation has for music and 
fashion... 'We want our OWN ....'.  If you were programming for speed 
you can't beat Assembler or Machine Code.  To enter data, crunching the 
numbers, and presenting the data to the user in a business environment 
I've seen nothing that beats COBOL (there are a few other languages that 
are as good).  The few arguments against COBOL that has much merit were 
not COBOL problems, rather problems related to the Mainframe.

Please don't flame me over this.  I'm old, haven't earned a penny from 
programming since 1992, but I try to keep somewhat current (and am 
failing at this).  Mr Dashwood and I share a common philosophy in that 
we use the best tool we have available for the job.  We had many choices 
in the days that IBM was the bad boy on the block, and I never quit 
looking for better 'tools'.  Third Party Sorts was big back then. 
Optimizing Compilers.  Various products to replace Report Writer. 
Panvalet Librarian, Westinghouse TP Monitor, anything was better than 
ICCF (at least to me).  Flat files, ISAM, VSAM, and SQL; each had their 
moments in computer history.  And the list goes on.

I was hired on as a COBOL programmer, but had many occasions to work in 
Assembler when Assembler was the right tool for that job.  The young 
hackers of the world might wish COBOL would just go away.  They should 
prepare themselves for disappointment.

On another subject:  Is it just me or do some of you sense that Mr 
DocWarf is in need of some help in anger management.  He seems to be 
easily... agitated.

enjoy your days...

sss
```

```
barcodes, write XML, and does SQL - in fact we do so.   But the
strengths of the language aren't on display when we do so.
```

```
> is a significant demand for programmers who use it.

Why would it need to be?

The days when a language needed to serve for a long time are gone. Ruby will 
do very nicely for the next few years and if it lasts longer, that's fine 
too. (I've seen some programmers already complaining about its limitations, 
but it was intended to be simple and it is difficult to have both power and 
simplicity that is unique.)

I am personally persuaded that NO current "computer programming" language 
will be important in 30 years time. I'm led to that conclusion not by 
inherent deficiencies in the extant languages, but by the fact that 
"computer programming" itself will be unrecognisable in 30 years time.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T02:35:23+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<78krleF1mqfinU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <78a19bF1ludk0U1@mid.individual.net> <I0VTl.5862$Gx.1307@newsfe04.iad> <78erb7F1lnmgaU1@mid.individual.net> <2op725d7a40b9fp238l1aj244in9ff7o69@4ax.com> <c3cd8$4a23f702$6216306a$18510@ALLTEL.NET>`

```
SeaSideSam wrote:
> Howard Brazee wrote:
>> On Sun, 31 May 2009 19:53:10 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> reason 'for' the argument.

OK, here's one:

1. COBOL was designed for a centralized, single-threaded architecture, with 
full control of all operations. (Yes, I know about privileged instructions, 
Sam, and I know about SVCs and ESTAEs... I'm old school, just like you - at 
least, there's part of me that is...:-) - for the sake of this argument, 
lets say that COBOL called on such facilities when it needed them...)

2. Client/server networks, and, especially, the Network of Networks 
(Internet and Web), are NOT such an architecture. There are many processing 
"cores" available; they are at different locations, the program no longer 
has central control (it is driven by "events" - you cannot decide when a 
user will click a mouse or drag and drop a control within the logic of your 
program... you can if you disable these facilities, but users get a bit 
pissed off with systems that don't work the way everything else does, or 
don't respond when they click a button...)

3.In a straight batch processing environment COBOL is completely at home; in 
a distributed environment , it isn't.

4. As the network developed, so new techniques evolved to deal with it. Some 
of these had already been under consideration, but were so ideally suited to 
networking that they just naturally flourished in that envirobment. The idea 
of "objects" with their own properites and behaviours was one such idea. If 
an object "knows" how to react to a certain event, it no longer needs a 
centralised program to tell it what to do. It can use the nearest available 
core, or it can be transported to a remote place where events are occurring 
that it is interested in (for example). In another variation of the 
scenario, events can occur and be processed by objects that are far removed 
from where the event was raised, or objects can activate behaviours that 
raise events far removed from the activating object. The point is that 
although objects mostly run and process on a local core, they don't HAVE to. 
It is a DIFFERENT paradigm, a DIFFERENT scenario,  a DIFFERENT Type of "data 
processing", not just because it is "event driven" but because the whole 
"behaviour" of the code is different from a procedural batch COBOL program.

5. By the time COBOL was extended to support Object Oriented Programming, it 
was already losing the race to newer languages (like Java) which had been 
designed from day one to support the concept of Classes and Objects. The 
result was: "Yes, you CAN write OO Classes in COBOL, but does it make sense 
to?" The answer is: "It depends..."

The above is not a reason for "getting rid of COBOL"; rather, it is a 
justification of why COBOL doesn't cut it any more. Now, you can reasonably 
argue that if all you are ever going to do is sequential batch processing 
you have no need of other tools, because COBOL can handle that very nicely 
thank you.

But the fact is that it isn't just IT that is changing. The world is 
changing, the marketplaces are changing and the methods of "doing business" 
are changing accordingly. While batch processing is still an important part 
of many businesses operations, there is serious doubt as to how long this 
will remain the case. Ultimately, the goal of a commercial IT system is to 
model the real state of the business in real time. In the real world 
transactions occur. For some time there simply wasn't the processing power 
available to fully process these transactions, so they were partially 
processed and clues and triggers were set up for overnight batch processing 
to complete the rest of the model. This is changing. Distributed systems can 
apply local processing cores to update remote databases (such as central 
corporate repositories), immediately in real time. (Transactions can run 
anywhere on the network and don't need to be collated into batches, sorted, 
and presented for sequential updating of master files or databases.) The 
dream of having the IT model reflect the real world at any given moment 
(almost to the millisecond) is becoming achievable. Better ways of "mining" 
and "warehousing" data are combining to obviate the necessity for batch 
processing to obtain historical data. Background processes can do this, not 
just in "overnight batch" but 24/7.

The bottom line is that it is "no contest" between COBOL and the "languages 
du jour", as far as network solutions are concerned. As networked solutions 
are becoming more and more popular, so COBOL is declining in popularity.

However, as long as both types of processing are still around, the serious 
professional programmer will need both types of tool, and knowledge of the 
implications for each.

And there is a very large amount of perfectly good code that CAN and SHOULD 
be salvaged...


> The only reason I can see that some want
> to get rid of COBOL is the same argument that each generation has for
> music and fashion... 'We want our OWN ....'.

That probably accounts for a small percentage of it. The real reasons are 
much more rational, as described above.

> If you were programming
> for speed you can't beat Assembler or Machine Code.  To enter data,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> related to the Mainframe.

Platforms were certainly a contributing factor, and the sea change in 
expectations and approaches can be traced back to the introduction of PCs 
and their subsequent displacing of centralized mainframes with client/server 
networks, dating from the late 1980s
>
> Please don't flame me over this.

This is a largely friendly community. We generally don't flame people any 
more; I think we grew out of it. Sometimes there are disagreements but it 
was found some time back that ad hominem arguments are not really productive 
(even if they can be amusing in retrospect :-)) I have been called things in 
this forum that no-one has ever said to my face in real life...:-)  ((It's 
easy to be "brave" from behind a keyboard... :-)) I'm still here, and I bear 
no rancour.

> I'm old, haven't earned a penny from
> programming since 1992, but I try to keep somewhat current (and am
> failing at this).  Mr Dashwood and I share a common philosophy in that
> we use the best tool we have available for the job.

I believe there are many others who also share this approach. My issue is 
with the ones who don't (that's OK, I respect their right to differ...), but 
then say there is no need to...like the languages of yesteryear being all 
that's necessary, or Holy Writ or something...and I must be unprofessional 
if I can't see that.


>We had many
> choices in the days that IBM was the bad boy on the block, and I
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> should prepare themselves for disappointment.

In actuality, very few "young hackers" either know or care about COBOL. It 
is not a language for hackers. Ironically, this is largely because it simply 
isn't powerful enough to be used for decent hacks. You certainly COULD write 
self replicating code in COBOL but it is tricky, laborious, and lengthy. 
Much easier to use a few lines of VB or C, or a script that writes other 
scripts.
>
> On another subject:  Is it just me or do some of you sense that Mr
> DocWarf is in need of some help in anger management.  He seems to be
> easily... agitated.

You may be confusing our own Doc Dwarf with his Klingon counterpart, Doc 
Worf.

Lay off the little feller...He hasn't had a drink in years...

Besides, if you give him a hard time he'll get Snow White to beat you up...


>
> enjoy your days...

You too. I think we share an affinity for the Seaside...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T11:00:26-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h0674712kt8@news5.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<78mbt0F1ktsrsU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <78a19bF1ludk0U1@mid.individual.net> <I0VTl.5862$Gx.1307@newsfe04.iad> <78erb7F1lnmgaU1@mid.individual.net> <2op725d7a40b9fp238l1aj244in9ff7o69@4ax.com> <c3cd8$4a23f702$6216306a$18510@ALLTEL.NET> <78krleF1mqfinU1@mid.individual.net> <h03ilh015tj@news7.newsguy.com> <78mbt0F1ktsrsU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> I never said "designed to run".

Fair enough. I was trying to extract something specific from "at home".

> OO languages (pedantically, "languages that support Object Oriented 
> Programming") are better suited to distributed environments than non-OO 
> languages because the Object Model suits itself to distribution better than 
> the procedural model.

Well, then, COBOL must be one of these "better suited" languages,
since it supports object-oriented programming.

To be honest, I'm rather dubious about the overall thesis. I can see
the argument for the usefulness of OO in some distributed programming,
simply because abstraction weakens coupling. But it's entirely
possible to achieve the same abstraction in a language with no direct
OO support, or without using the OO features of a language - and in
fact this is done all the time. Nearly all the PHP-based apps I've
seen make minimal use of PHP's OO syntax, for example. Yes, they'd be
much cleaner if they were written using a polymorphic MVC design; but
their developers simply haven't found that a compelling benefit.

So while it might be convincingly argued that OO features make it
easier to write many kinds of distributed applications in clean,
robust, maintainable ways, in practice that argument appears to be
relatively inconsequential. We've been saying similar things about
dynamic functional languages since the early 1960s, and most
applications are still written in static compiled procedural languages
(with or without OO sugar).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T15:22:18+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<78osvcF1mv8cpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <78a19bF1ludk0U1@mid.individual.net> <I0VTl.5862$Gx.1307@newsfe04.iad> <78erb7F1lnmgaU1@mid.individual.net> <2op725d7a40b9fp238l1aj244in9ff7o69@4ax.com> <c3cd8$4a23f702$6216306a$18510@ALLTEL.NET> <78krleF1mqfinU1@mid.individual.net> <h03ilh015tj@news7.newsguy.com> <78mbt0F1ktsrsU1@mid.individual.net> <h0674712kt8@news5.newsguy.com>`

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> since it supports object-oriented programming.

Yes, OO COBOL is indeed more useful for distributed environments.

I found this out by using it for 10 years. I still have a number of OO COBOL 
components that are running in newer environments... (have a look at:
http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21/S2NTestServer.aspx
for just ONE example...)

Unfortunately, although OO COBOL is excellent, it simply suffers by 
comparison with more modern OO languages, which had object orientation 
designed into them from the start. However, that is a different argument and 
it will be coloured by personal preferences. I have been very happy to port 
my existing inventory of COBOL components to the .NET environment. They run 
perfectly well with C#, using Interop Services and I am relieved that it 
wasn't necessary to redevelop stuff that took years to write.
>
> To be honest, I'm rather dubious about the overall thesis. I can see
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> their developers simply haven't found that a compelling benefit.

Can/should we judge the efficacy of an argument about distribution and OO by 
relating it to a sample of PHP? :-)

There are many reasons why programmers don't always take the "best" 
approach. Some will argue Occam's razor and do the minimum that they can, 
some are simply not aware of wider implications in what they do 
("polymorphic MVCs" f'rinstance, are not EVERYBODY's cup of tea...) The 
point is that there are millions of people programming computers, who DON'T 
necessarily have the in-depth Academic background that you have Michael. 
Nevertheless, they achieve results.

I did not seek to imply (in fact I have stated exactly the opposite, 
explicitly) that you CANNOT do distributed computing in procedural code; 
what I am suggesting is that OO code is better for it. You noted yourself 
the fact that abstraction weakens coupling, but that is just one aspect of 
it. Encapsulation too, weakens coupling, and then there is the benefit of 
reuse with components being instanced locally and remotely as and when 
needed. It is not so easy to achieve that with procedural approaches.
>
> So while it might be convincingly argued that OO features make it
> easier to write many kinds of distributed applications in clean,
> robust, maintainable ways, in practice that argument appears to be
> relatively inconsequential.

It is if you ignore it. :-)

That's kind of like saying: "Well yes, our local site Programming Standards 
would definitely make it easier and better to develop code, but nobody pays 
a mind to them, so they are really inconsequential."

I know, from my own experience, that applying the concepts I have outlined 
works extremely well, just as the Programming Standards (even and especially 
for COBOL) which I have written in various places, have been well received. 
These days, "Best Practice" is a lot more prevalent than it was back in the 
Wild West of the early days of computing. It isn't quite as necessary to 
spell things out because "everybody knows" already...Programming Standards 
from the last century have become "site guidelines" in this one. By the same 
token, many more people recognize the value of an OO approach for 
distributed network programming both on the desktop and on the web. 
(Notwithstanding your PHP sample... I don't use PHP  so I really can't 
comment. I DO use ASP.NET and I can assure you OO plays a large part in it. 
The framework Classes provide a rich library of functionality for Web pages 
AND desktop applications.

Fortunately, it isn't just me... I'm seeing more and more people interested 
in leveraging their legacy COBOL and realizing that OO is a very useful tool 
for doing it. It simply ISN'T enough to just recompile everything for .NET 
and many people are starting to recognise that.


> We've been saying similar things about
> dynamic functional languages since the early 1960s, and most
> applications are still written in static compiled procedural languages
> (with or without OO sugar).

Not sure who "we" is in the above... :-) (I certainly haven't...)

But I do take your point. There is talk that a "dynamic functional" release 
of C# is in the pipeline. Personally, I'll get excited if it gives me more 
functionality, but I really couldn't care less about whether a compiler is 
static or not (at least, not at this stage of my development; maybe when I 
have learned much more, I'll care more; right now, I just want the code to 
work.

Pete.
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> designed into them from the start.

I tend to agree, actually. I think the same applies to C++, which can
in theory be used to write very elegant programs, but rarely is used
that way in practice.

On the other hand, OCaml is an ML variant with OO facilities added on,
and there the combination is less awkward.

>> To be honest, I'm rather dubious about the overall thesis. I can see
>> the argument for the usefulness of OO in some distributed programming,
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> relating it to a sample of PHP? :-)

Well, I was trying to make a couple of arguments there (and collapsed
them into one not terribly coherent paragraph). One was that, on the
one hand, it's possible to achieve OO abstractions in languages
without specific OO features; the other was that programmers writing
distributed applications in OO-enabled languages often fail to use OO
practices. PHP applications offer many examples of the latter.

PHP is a fairly awful language, partly by design (serious craziness in
some of the primitive operations, a complete mess of library functions
adapted willy-nilly from various sources, etc), but also partly
because few PHP developers seem to want to take the time to write
decent code in it. And I believe that problem is widespread, and not
specific to PHP: while languages that offer high-level abstractions
*can* be used to write better distributed (and monolithic)
applications, they rarely *are* used that way. So that advantage
carries little weight in the marketplace.

> There are many reasons why programmers don't always take the "best" 
> approach. Some will argue Occam's razor and do the minimum that they can, 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Nevertheless, they achieve results.

Pretty much the point I was trying to make. Languages like COBOL won't
be displaced because other languages are better suited to "modern"
needs - simply because most developers don't select languages based on
how well they're suited to the problem at hand.

> That's kind of like saying: "Well yes, our local site Programming Standards 
> would definitely make it easier and better to develop code, but nobody pays 
> a mind to them, so they are really inconsequential."

Yep. I believe that's how most software development works today.
Developers are surrounded by good ideas they don't use. And
consequently most software is rubbish.

> I know, from my own experience, that applying the concepts I have outlined 
> works extremely well, just as the Programming Standards (even and especially 
> for COBOL) which I have written in various places, have been well received. 
> These days, "Best Practice" is a lot more prevalent than it was back in the 
> Wild West of the early days of computing.

Perhaps. That's not what I see. But I don't have a significant sample
of empirical data; I'm going by the code I see, the software I use,
and proxies like vulnerability and bug reports (from BUGTRAQ, RISKS,
and similar venues).

> It simply ISN'T enough to just recompile everything for .NET 
> and many people are starting to recognise that.

Sure. I'm all for people investigating the resources available to them.

>> We've been saying similar things about
>> dynamic functional languages since the early 1960s, and most
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Not sure who "we" is in the above... :-) (I certainly haven't...)

Oh, computer-science academics, researchers investigating
software-quality issues - those sorts of people. Folks like McCarthy
and Backus and Key.

> But I do take your point. There is talk that a "dynamic functional" release 
> of C# is in the pipeline.

Not surprising, since C# already has lambdas for LINQ, and since
Microsoft already offers F#. The F# research team apparently built an
experimental enhanced CLR with better support for dynamic languages,
so this was probably mostly a matter of the C# folks deciding what
capabilities they wanted to incorporate into the official .NET Framework.

> Personally, I'll get excited if it gives me more 
> functionality, but I really couldn't care less about whether a compiler is 
> static or not (at least, not at this stage of my development; maybe when I 
> have learned much more, I'll care more; right now, I just want the code to 
> work.

Sure. And whether it gives you more functionality really depends on
what problems you're trying to solve and what approaches you want to
take in solving them.

When I write applications in Javascript (which is a very nice
prototyping language for a lot of purposes; web page bells and
whistles don't interest me, but Javascript is surprisingly handy for
prototyping things like natural-language heuristic analyzers), I use
dynamic closures and function composition all over the place. There
are other perfectly good ways to do what I'm doing; those are just
techniques that I'm comfortable with and that let me write highly
abstract, concise, expressive code.

Similarly, in C#, one person might use partial classes where another
might use discrete classes with a wrapper; and perhaps someday someone
might use a dynamically-extended class in that same situation.
```

```
>Pete.

Well it might be a bit of a stretch to say piezoelectronics have
anything to do with telephones but PC Magazine defines
piezoelectronics as "The property of certain crystals that causes them
to produce voltage when a mechanical pressure is applied to them such
as sound vibrations. This technique is used to build crystal
microphones, phonograph cartridges and strain gauges, all of which
turn mechanical movement into voltage."  

This could be the same technique used in early model telephone mouth
pieces, but I doubt that is what is used today.  But on that note,
there are plenty of folks who can barely dial a cell phone much less
upgrade its ringtones.  Would they be considered "telephone
illiterate"?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 18)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-06-08T15:41:13+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h0jbep$j1t$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<78a19bF1ludk0U1@mid.individual.net> <h0f9ob$32k$3@reader1.panix.com> <790q7fF1ncghkU1@mid.individual.net> <lmon25lm7nnh28u5ev6n0ocnt08kb4erei@4ax.com>`

```
In article <lmon25lm7nnh28u5ev6n0ocnt08kb4erei@4ax.com>,
SkippyPB  <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
>On Sun, 7 Jun 2009 15:24:29 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
><dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>illiterate"?

Precisely my point, Mr Wiegand... although the technology I chose to 
demonstrate it might be a bit archaic.  I recall being taught that the 
'microphone' part of a Olde-Style telephone handset consisted of a 
crystals which, in response to the pressure of the sound waves against 
them, generated varying electric outputs that were amplified and converted 
to the sound that came out of the earpiece on the other end of the line... 
in a way, a microphone was the same as a speaker except for the direction 
of electronic/sound flow.

Things may have changed a bit with these newfangled gadgets folks wear on 
their hips, flipping them open like Captain Quark of the Starship 
Intercourse - or whatever it was called - and I do not know if cellular 
phones rely on this same principle.  But... the principle of the 
principle, the 'I can get what I want/need out of the device without 
knowing much about the innards', is what I was trying to emphasise.  Folks 
didn't need to know much about how an electron 'gun' would spray the back 
of a phosphor-coated class tube in order to watch the latest episode of 
'As The Stomach Turns' and yet they got what they wanted... did 
'television literacy' (whatever that is) have a necessary role?

Yes, in that you had to plug it into a working electric socket, hook up 
the antenna ('Little to the left... liiiiiitle more... all right, now hold 
it right there!'), turn the switch on and adjust the volume, maybe diddle 
with the vertical-hold knob so John and Mary didn't flip-flip-flip... but 
what about the winding of wires needed to make the setting for Channel 
VIII differ from that for Channel IX?  (it was a long time ago)

Decades back I remember having discussions about such matters while under 
the influence of alcohol... perhaps that is a reason for my considering 
them to be such...

... loaded terms.

DD
```

```
We certainly don't have the "automobile literacy" now than we had back
before they added all of those computers to cars.   And being
"computer literate" doesn't help me take care of my car.

But then, cars last a lot longer with a lot less need for me to know
how things work.   I don't anticipate ever putting on a manual choke
again on a car.   Good thing, as now it would involve replacing a
computer or two (although that is simple - if expensive).   But I
won't have the need either.

In our industry, we don't need to wire boards anymore.   Being skilled
at assembler language is no longer a high demand skill.   Gradually,
our computer skills should be less involved with the hardware, and
more with business needs.

That said - we have lots more people working in the black box of stuff
our users don't see than ever before.   There are relatively fewer
applications programmers, and more people in systems, security,
communications, privacy, etc.
```

```
:-)

There is so much about COBOL that is good, and useful, it might make sense 
to provide a "less verbose" version of OO COBOL.

I remember many decades ago ICL had a product called "Compact COBOL" which 
used shortened forms of verbs and didn't implement the full language 
facilities. (I suppose you could think of it as a kind of "COBOLised 
Easytrieve".

The trouble was that the COBOL people were horrified by it (it wasn't 
verbose enough) and the people who used were frustrated by it (it was too 
verbose, compared to other tools available at the time...)

It is very hard (some would say "impossible") to be all things to all 
people...

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T10:17:47-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<jt7d25pmg09fvnfc2nh17t86q35r63vs4q@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <c3cd8$4a23f702$6216306a$18510@ALLTEL.NET> <78krleF1mqfinU1@mid.individual.net> <tsva25d56nqjai51n1hh8fi850hbp62u97@4ax.com> <h04bg5$svm$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Tue, 2 Jun 2009 23:09:57 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:


>>>And there is a very large amount of perfectly good code that CAN and SHOULD 
>>>be salvaged...
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>need those?

I tend to observe that too often, that time and money saved is
illusionary.   We are writing a bunch of conversion programs in our
shop.   The other day I took over the conversion program someone else
cloned from a previous spaghetti written program.   Today I was asked
to determine whether some logic was correct - someone on the other
side didn't think there were enough populated data for a field, but
couldn't find a wrong instance.    It is taking me a significant
effort to check it out.

The programs I wrote for this, I wrote, avoiding the temptation to do
quick and dirty modifications of previous code.   Even though we don't
expect them to be around long, it makes answering questions like this
easy.

And, you know - it doesn't take long to write a new program from
scratch, not if I know the requirements.    And if I don't know the
requirements, well, understanding whether the clone and copy program
works correctly is a chore.

>>We don't melt down hammers to make saws.   I never have copied
>>much CoBOL moving from one environment to another - the most we have
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>might need a bit of looking at.  No need to rewrite, just copy.

This particular example is so simple that it doesn't take 3 minutes to
write and verify.    I don't see a big savings in searching for old
code and cutting and pasting it into my code.

Complex code being copied (or better yet encapsulated), has an
advantage - provided we understand what it is doing and what I want it
to do sufficiently.    But with such understanding, writing new code
is quick and easy as well.

Going to a new system has always entailed significant changes.   When
the old process needs to be modified to fit the new process - the old
process needs to be understood, tested, and debugged anyway.   Why not
just write it to fit the new process from the beginning?

How long does it take to write a new program?   For an competent
programmer who understands what is needed - not long at all.   It's
not the writing that takes time and effort, it's the analysis and
testing.   Which we have to do with old or new code alike.
```

```
<http://groups.google.com/group/comp.lang.cobol/msg/183ad8a365182dc6?dmode=source>

--begin quoted text:

Years on back I would ask the question 'How long does it take to get a cup 
of coffee and some papers photocopied?'... and my response would be 'No, 
it takes about an hour and a half.  First, the coffee machine's emptied to 
its last sixteenth-of-an-inch of boiled sludge and you have to wash out 
the carafe and get another pot started.  Then you walk across the floor to 
the photocopy machine... and find that it is down.  You take the elevator 
up to the next floor, stand in the line for the available photocopy 
machine, put your originals on the glass... and find out you've forgotten 
two sheets.  You wait for the elevator, get back to your floor, stop by 
the coffee machine and find it empty again.  You get another batch of 
coffee running, fetch your missing sheets, wait for the elevator, wait in 
line, make your copies, wait for the elevator, *this* time you're lucky 
and there's a cup of coffee left... but there's no sugar, you have to go 
to the supply cabinet the *other* way across the floor and beg the Old 
Lady With The Key for office supplies.  You get the sugar, rush back to 
find that last cup of coffee still in the pot - a day of miracles! - and 
make yourself some mud... in your annoyance, of course, not making the 
third pot of coffee and prepetuating a bit of the cause of your own delay 
to others.

A cup of coffee and a few pages photocopied... an hour and a half, right 
there.'

--end quoted text

So... how long does it take to write a program?  I'll let you know as soon 
as the network comes back up.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Realistic reuse WAS Re: You know what they say about statistics and statisticians

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T16:25:29+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<78p0lrF1m17q0U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<KWJTl.26990$Xo1.5087@en-nntp-07.dc1.easynews.com> <c3cd8$4a23f702$6216306a$18510@ALLTEL.NET> <78krleF1mqfinU1@mid.individual.net> <tsva25d56nqjai51n1hh8fi850hbp62u97@4ax.com> <h04bg5$svm$1@reader1.panix.com> <jt7d25pmg09fvnfc2nh17t86q35r63vs4q@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Tue, 2 Jun 2009 23:09:57 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> just write it to fit the new process from the beginning?

Because you are dealing with lines of code. Every line you DON'T have to 
write is a saving... Covered in depth below.
>
> How long does it take to write a new program?   For an competent
> programmer who understands what is needed - not long at all.   It's
> not the writing that takes time and effort, it's the analysis and
> testing.   Which we have to do with old or new code alike.

I followed your arguments with interest, Howard, and although I see what you 
are saying, my heart is still with the Doc on this... :-)

Yes, cost savings can be illusionary.

Yes, there WILL be a lot of work in any conversion.

Yes, sometimes, given the conditions you carefully noted, it is "just as 
quick" to write something new. (Really it comes down to being "just as 
quick" as doing the searching to find and understand the existing code; it 
can never be quicker to write something new if the existing code does what 
it should and is right at your fingertips...)

But, even given the above, I find Doc's points more compelling:

1. In general, money CAN be saved by salvage and recycling.
2. (Quoting from Doc's earlier post on this:

"I have move from environment to environment and rarely copied a file
structure... a date routine, an interest calculation routine, a routine to
calculate when and where to wrap around the alphabet... these I have
copied and used again and again.  I may have a few years on me, true, but
since I've started coding January has had 31 days, February at least 28
(and the need to check for leap years), March 31 days... and April 32
might need a bit of looking at.  No need to rewrite, just copy."

That is the nub of encapsulating and re-using functionality. It isn't just 
about dates; there are THOUSANDS of functions in any business that could be 
similarly isolated and re-used.

(Next time you have a COBOL listing in front of you, have a look at the 
PERFORMED routines... Why were they PERFORMed? Because the programmer 
realised the same code would need to be executed from more than one place 
and, quite properly, put it in a form where it could be shared. But what 
about all the other programs that use the same functionality, or minor 
variations on it? They all have similar (or even the same ...especially if 
written by the same programmer) code copied into them. It was "too small" to 
warrant putting in a separate module, but it was too frequently used to code 
it inline every time it was needed.)

So we end up with the same functionality propagated and perpetuated 
throughout the system(s), using a different interface in every program, with 
different datanames.

This is partly because of the historic separation between programming and 
analysis. The programmer is responsible for getting debugged working code 
written to a schedule, by following a specification provided by an analyst. 
The analyst simply boilerplates the function into each program spec. so it 
is hardly any wonder it gets propagated at code level.

If the analysis methodology is different (as it would be on an Agile site, 
for example) the results will be different. Joint Application Development 
workshops mean that programmer, analyst, and client ALL look at the kind of 
functions which will be required by the proposed system. The programmer is 
no longer blinkered to a program spec. but understands the business 
requirement. Now it is possible to look at what are likely to be common 
functions OUTSIDE of the context of a specific program. Major Classes 
(Customer, Product, etc.) can be identified and populated with properties 
derived from various use cases which involve them. In the same way, 
functions common to a number of business processes can be identified.

Now, you COULD (and this seems to be what you are advocating, Howard) 
say:"Let's forget about the existing stuff, we can just write the functions 
and classes we have identified, from scratch, and get a completely new (in 
every sense of the word) system.

Then the programmer speaks up and says: "We already have a lot of this 
functionality encoded (s/he knows, because s/he wrote it...), shouldn't we 
try and salvage some of this?" It could save time, effort, and, 
consequentially, money.

Or, it might not, if the factors you identified are in place and it is very 
difficult to identify and retrieve the code.

Even in the scenario I described with what is fundamentally the same 
function being PERFORMed from all over a system, I still think it is worth 
salvaging it. You only need to use ONE copy of it as a template for a new 
class.

Doc made the point that even when moving within the SAME TECHNICAL 
ENVIRONMENT (same programming language, no change in analysis methodology) 
he has found it useful to copy existing code. (So have I, back in "the old 
days", and the OO approach is really just an extension of that experience... 
encapsulate it into an OO COM Class and you have it forever, even if the 
next place you want to use it is NOT a COBOL shop...)

You made a very good point that it is the analysis that takes the time, not 
the programming. But it is the ongoing maintenance that takes even more time 
than the analysis and programming put together.

Encapsulating and re-using functionality can DRAMATICALLY not only reduce 
the time it takes to do maintenance, but it can reduce the amount of 
maintenance required and the amount of regression testing required.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Where is CoBOL

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-02T11:40:58-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h03ili115tj@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<j7ba25hojc9v0l9ohu0otkppf0eiqrirgj@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<j7ba25hojc9v0l9ohu0otkppf0eiqrirgj@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> In this language comparison?
> 
> http://gmarceau.qc.ca/blog/2009/05/speed-size-and-dependability-of.html

The author begins by confusing language and implementation - a
distinction which is important later in the article - and it doesn't
get much better. There's no serious acknowledgment that languages may
be better suited for some purposes rather than others, for example.
And he appears to assume that the implementation of a particular
solution in the data set represents a typical or ideal solution and
implementation in that language, when it may simply be that no one has
supplied the site with a good solution or implementation for that
particular benchmark-language pair.

And while the results are interesting, the author's claims for them
are rather overstated, particularly given the tremendously arbitrary
nature of his data and the subjective aspects of his methodology.

To answer Howard's question: the benchmarks here aren't well-suited to
COBOL. It's debatable whether the benchmarks are chosen well even for
their ostensible purpose - for example, they're run on a very
restricted class of machine, and the rules require that
implementations "avoid library use", which seems completely contrary
to the stated mission of the project. (What are libraries for? To
reduce code size and make good implementations of common functions
available. What's the benchmark project trying to measure? Code size
and performance.)

The benchmarks that I looked at in the suite are CPU-bound
compute-intensive tasks. They're all short programs. I don't think
they say much about how expressive a language is - there's just not
enough to do. Real applications are seldom a single page of code in
any language.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Where is CoBOL

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2009-06-02T12:55:50-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7ata25tosk1pgi23o68366nsi1vhl4jve5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<j7ba25hojc9v0l9ohu0otkppf0eiqrirgj@4ax.com> <h03ili115tj@news7.newsguy.com>`

```

<mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:

>COBOL. It's debatable whether the benchmarks are chosen well even for
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>any language.

And certainly there are different efficiency needs in running p-code
like code on a PC than in running compiled code in a central computer.
Besides, CoBOL is as efficient as its compiler makes it to be.   How
do we compare XML with compiled Cobol?  And why?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Where is CoBOL

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T11:26:17-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h0674822kt8@news5.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<7ata25tosk1pgi23o68366nsi1vhl4jve5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<j7ba25hojc9v0l9ohu0otkppf0eiqrirgj@4ax.com> <h03ili115tj@news7.newsguy.com> <7ata25tosk1pgi23o68366nsi1vhl4jve5@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> How do we compare XML with compiled Cobol?  And why?

There's no point in comparing XML with COBOL; XML isn't a programming
language. It's a data representation.

But - and I think this is the point we're both making - comparing
COBOL with another programming language in the fashion described in
this article isn't terribly useful either. There's no obvious
relationship between the lines of code needed to calculate the
Mandelbrot set, or the time it takes to do so, and the requirements of
most real applications.

Even the author's key theoretical innovation - the shape of the plot
of each implementation's results on the graph - can at best only show
whether the language is well-suited for all or only some of the
benchmarks in the set. That's one possible criterion for a programming
language, but not a necessary one. In many cases a domain-specific
language, or one with strengths in particular areas, is a better choice.

I have always (since I first studied them, in the mid-80s) been an
advocate of highly-expressive programming languages, even though most
of my work is in C and COBOL buys my bread. I was pleased to see some
old favorites like OCaml and Lua do well in the article's comparisons.
But it's not worth getting excited about. There are many, many
programming languages with features that make them arguably superior
to the dominant crop. There have been since the earliest days of
high-level languages; LISP is only a few years younger than FORTRAN.
But most software is still terrible, a bug-ridden mess. Better
languages have not fixed that yet.
```

#### ↳ Re: Where is CoBOL

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T17:36:51-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<edbaa1b2-b833-4bbc-9e3b-09fde05c999a@z19g2000vbz.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<j7ba25hojc9v0l9ohu0otkppf0eiqrirgj@4ax.com>`

```
I'll opine that a capable, freely available COBOL hasn't yet had time
to take root, so developers that would feel the need to scratch the
shootout itch have not yet surfaced.  OpenCOBOL could be that ticket
to ride for gaining momentum in "at home" COBOL coding.  TinyCOBOL too
perhaps, but OpenCOBOL is becoming quite the robust system, even
though it lacks Object COBOL features for now.

It's a nice star chart though, given what it is.  But the author
seeing verbosity as a "bad thing" might hurt COBOL's chances at being
placed near the sweet spot of the ideal corner. I prefer grokking
through code once and find verbosity a boon, when compared to, say, 12
characters of Perl code that can take half an hour and four
experiments to come to grips with.   ;)

One coder's expressionism if oft another's puzzle.

Cheers,
Brian

On Jun 2, 9:45 am, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:

>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> - James Madison
```

```
Paul H wrote:
> I received this answer in the Excel newsgroup.  Does COBOL have an
> equivalent capability?

The poster (John) is probably correct.

Fujitsu COBOL has a "Yield" function that gives control back to Windows so 
it can deal with just such an eventuality. (It "yields" the cpu back to 
Windows and prevents COBOL from locking up the entire system.)

You should search the Micro Focus docs for a similar function. If you can't 
find one you will need to use the Windows API...

Really just a lot simpler to write in VB or C#... :-)

Of course, it might be that your output spreadsheet is gagging at 1000 
lines.  Have you checked that page breaks are being inserted? Have you 
looked at the file that is created? Is anything "cruel or unusual" happening 
around the 1000 line mark?

Pete.
```

```
In COBOL mainframe CICS coding you can execute a CICS command that
puts the program to sleep for whatever interval you code.  That would
allow other tasks running in CICS to do things they were waiting on an
interrupt to do.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Speed drops after 1,000 records?

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T12:04:19-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0ba8e3c1-400b-4977-ab07-cf2dec795b3c@3g2000yqk.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Jun 3, 3:54 pm, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> wrote:
> I received this answer in the Excel newsgroup.  Does COBOL have an
> equivalent capability?
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Don't know if Cobol has an similar statement.

Cobol does not have a 'statement' to do this, but may be able to CALL
a Windows routine to do this. It is, however, probably pointless to do
so.

What the 'doevents' is likely to do is to let other programs display
by 'yielding' the message queue and CPU which will slow down the Cobol
program.

If you are doing more than 1000 rows then Excel is probably the wrong
tool. In fact for most things Excel is probably the wrong tool, but is
the one used because they can.

It is likely that beyond row 1024 Excel needs to change the way it
works and/or the recalculation on every change takes longer.

Turn off recalculation and redisplay before adding the rows and turn
these on again after all have been added and stop the CPU cycles being
thrown away to no purpose.

> John
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> fix this?  TIA, Paul
```

```
Hi Riplin,

Turning off the recalc sounds reasonable.  As I said in my 5/2/09 4:55 pm 
query, the "One Who Signs The Timesheets" wants to provide their many users 
with spreadsheets that have bold headings, correctly sized columns, etc. 
Now I have to find an INVOKE statement that will accomplish this.  I have 
not been able to find a comprehensive list of INVOKE statements for my Net 
Express 3.1 version of MicroFocus COBOL.  Thanks for the tip.

Paul

<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:0ba8e3c1-400b-4977-ab07-cf2dec795b3c@3g2000yqk.googlegroups.com...
On Jun 3, 3:54 pm, "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoS...@att.net> wrote:
> I received this answer in the Excel newsgroup. Does COBOL have an
> equivalent capability?
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Don't know if Cobol has an similar statement.

Cobol does not have a 'statement' to do this, but may be able to CALL
a Windows routine to do this. It is, however, probably pointless to do
so.

What the 'doevents' is likely to do is to let other programs display
by 'yielding' the message queue and CPU which will slow down the Cobol
program.

If you are doing more than 1000 rows then Excel is probably the wrong
tool. In fact for most things Excel is probably the wrong tool, but is
the one used because they can.

It is likely that beyond row 1024 Excel needs to change the way it
works and/or the recalculation on every change takes longer.

Turn off recalculation and redisplay before adding the rows and turn
these on again after all have been added and stop the CPU cycles being
thrown away to no purpose.

> John
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> fix this? TIA, Paul
```

```
Paul H wrote:
> Hi Riplin,
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL.  Thanks for the tip.

Paul,

You may be misunderstanding the use of "invoke".

There ISN'T a "comprehensive list of INVOKE statements" for your version of 
COBOL or any other version.

Could you find a comprehesive list of CALL statements?

Invoke is the OO equivalent of a CALL.  You CALL a callable module, you 
INVOKE an instance of an OO Class.

Excel is an Automation Server. That means it is a COM component that 
complies with COM interfacing. (Automation servers comply with the Component 
Object Model (COM)). The functions you have been "INVOKE"ing are all METHODS 
of the Excel Automation server Class.

You can think of them as "Built in functions" that are part of Excel. 
Whenever you interface with Excel (by program or by keyboard/mouse) it does 
what it does by invoking these methods internally.

Using the COM interface is like having access to Excel internals.

If you were going to look for a "comprehensive list" of the functions 
available, you would look in Excel, NOT in COBOL.

(COM methods can be invoked from MANY languages...)

Some time ago (March 26th, 2009) I suggested you use an Object Browser to 
get the list of all the Methods available (there are hundreds of them for 
Excel and it shares some of them with other Office products), and I even 
gave some detailed instructions (blow-by-blow) on how to activate and use 
the Object Browser that is available with Excel and other Office Automation 
servers (in fact, anything that can support VBA). You never acknowledged the 
post so I guess I wasted my time.

I would really appreciate it if you had mailed me privately if you were 
having problems and would gladly have walked you through it.

Here's a reference to the original post:

http://groups.google.co.nz/group/comp.lang.cobol/browse_thread/thread/ebeb56f60a84e5d5/cdc6540ddd6ff475?hl=en&lnk=gst&q=object+browser#cdc6540ddd6ff475

You'll understand if there is no further help forthcoming from me.(I simply 
don't have time to waste; it isn't personal)

Pete.
```

## Thread: How many records in a line sequentila file? — 20 msgs

### How many records in a line sequentila file?

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T00:05:17-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Is there a way to ask exactly how many records are in a file, without having 
to open the file, then read and count the records?  TIA, Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: How many records in a line sequentila file?

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T13:10:45+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h05sol$2rj$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
In article <4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>,
Paul H <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote:
>Is there a way to ask exactly how many records are in a file, without having 
>to open the file, then read and count the records?  TIA, Paul 

Getting a solution that Actually Works For You is usually facilitated by 
mentioning things like the Operating System under which you are working 
and the version of COBOL with which you're coding... but all that aside...

... if there exists in your environment a way to determine the size of 
file in question then it should not be too difficult to call/invoke that 
particular subroutine/API/method/object and cobble together something like 
DIVIDE WS-SIZE-OF-FILE BY FUNCTION LENGTH (RECORD-SIZE) GIVING 
WS-NUMBER-OF-RECORDS...

... or something like that.  Be warned that a) I coded the above from 
memory and consulting The Manual might save a bit of debugging time and b) 
depending on Stuff the call/invoke might not give an accurate result if 
the file has not been CLOSEd.

DD
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How many records in a line sequentila file?

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T08:19:42-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3NuVl.31040$Ws1.11130@nlpi064.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <h05sol$2rj$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:h05sol$2rj$1@reader1.panix.com...
> In article <4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>,
> Paul H <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote:
>>Is there a way to ask exactly how many records are in a file, without 
>>having
>>to open the file, then read and count the records?  TIA, Paul

> ... if there exists in your environment a way to determine the size of
> file in question then it should not be too difficult to call/invoke that
> particular subroutine/API/method/object and cobble together something like
> DIVIDE WS-SIZE-OF-FILE BY FUNCTION LENGTH (RECORD-SIZE) GIVING
> WS-NUMBER-OF-RECORDS...

While that works for files with fixed record length, in general the answer 
for the 'generic' line sequential ("text") file is no.  BTW, in above 
formula, don't forget to include the size of the record separator in the 
record length before doing the match.

The very definition of "line sequential" files suggests they are used when 
you ALWAYS access the data starting at the beginning and read it in order; 
otherwise you would have selected a different "ORGANIZATION"  when you 
designed it.

Of course, there's no reason you could not maintain another file containing 
the current record count....

MCM
```

```
01 record-1          PIC X(80).

MOVE ALL "1" TO record-1
WRITE record-1
MOVE ALL "2" TO record-2
WRITE record-2
```

```
Oh Dear, trusting to the argument from Authority and not actually thinking 
about it? I shun your quoted reference, having used Line Sequential files 
for over 20 years and knowing how they actually operate. (I am not saying 
the reference is "wrong", just that you may not be familiar with this type 
of file organization...)

> but if there are no trailing
> blanks what is there to vary in length?

The individual string which comprises each record.

Consider...

This is a string
Here's another one
They're NOT the same length
Even AFTER the trailing blanks are stripped out
or not
Of course, any number of given records
COULD be exactly the same length
like this one (24 chars)
and this one  (24 chars)
but that would be purely coincidental and just a case where the variable 
length
happened to be the same variable

HTH,

Pete.
```

```
the rightness or wrongness of the manual to that system, as has
already been pointed out several times.

The question you asked was 'what is there to vary in length'. The
answer already provided is: the record (01) area; and/or the implicit
record area of, say, a WRITE .. FROM. But this may vary in _different_
implementations.

For example ICL DRS20 CIS COBOL (ICL TP R92584/00) states (7.2.2) that
"Line Sequential files always map onto DRS20 SAM file." These are
nominally fixed length records without terminators but are Run Length
Encoded and maintained by the DRX OS with record header information.

So your manual would be wrong if it were describing _that_ system.

The Fujitsu Compilers (up to version 7 at least) on Windows and Linux
also, by default, do not strip trailing spaces from Line Sequential
files.

So your manual would be wrong if it were describing _that_ system.

You would be wrong if you were asserting that the manual you quoted
from is a general description on Line Sequential files on all systems.
If you were not asserting that then why you asking for a reference for
a _different_ system that made you manual 'wrong' ?

Your rather tedious arguments just shows that Peter's (and my)
experience is rather wider than yours.

LINE SEQUENTIAL is not part of ANS85 (though it is of 02) and is an
extension in all pre-02 compilers. Regardless of that, the
representation of the file on media is implementation dependent and
will vary as required by the system.

> [snip]
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> then I believe I'll prefer losing just about any day.

You had already lost the game before your alleged 'see and raise'
because Peter had 'seen you' with his """I am not saying the reference
is "wrong"""", and all you could show was a singleton manual against
Peter's 'full house'.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: How many records in a line sequentila file?

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-03T11:33:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h0674932kt8@news5.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<h05sol$2rj$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <h05sol$2rj$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <4a2604c0$0$23751$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>,
> Paul H <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net> wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> and the version of COBOL with which you're coding...

Indeed, it will depend on the representation of a line-sequential file
in this environment, and whether the metadata in question (number of
records) is maintained at all.

In Unix and Windows, for example, "line-sequential" files are
typically just OS files, using standard filesystems, which keep such
files as byte streams and have no notion of record delimiters. The
only size metadata those systems keep is the total file length, and
the records are variable-length, so there is no  alternative to
reading the file and counting the records.

Other environments may represent line-sequential files in other ways,
and keep other metadata.
```

## Thread: FUNCTION LENGTH — 13 msgs

### FUNCTION LENGTH

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T09:59:20-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
Hmm, this surprised me.  I just tried this:

subtract function length(fjs-root-seglen)
         from fjs-root-seglen            
         giving datalen                  

And got this.

IGYPS2095-S EXPECTED A NUMERIC DATA ITEM OR A NUMERIC LITERAL, BUT FOUND
"FUNCTION".
            THE "SUBTRACT" STATEMENT WAS DISCARDED.

IBM ENTERPRISE COBOL FOR Z/OS  3.4.1

It works fine in a compute statement:

compute fjs-root-seglen = function length(fjs-root-segment)
                        - datalen


I thought that FUNCTION LENGTH was allowed anywhere that a numeric data item
or numeric literal was allowed.  Seems strange that it's not allowed here.

The IBM extension LENGTH OF fjs-root-seglen works fine.

Probably it follows the standard, though I can't imagine why the standard
would disallow that usage...  (Too lazy to actually look at the standard at
the moment to try to explain it.)

Frank
```

#### ↳ Re: FUNCTION LENGTH

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T16:42:14+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h08th6$ovt$1@reader1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
In article <4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>,
Frank Swarbrick <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote:
>Hmm, this surprised me.  I just tried this:
>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>or numeric literal was allowed.  Seems strange that it's not allowed here.

What is Life without a bit of Mystery?  More slogging through The Manual 
(an IBM Manual, of course, being defined as 'a set of 26 chapters, each of 
which assumes intimate knowledge of the other 25') gives 
<http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3lr10/7.1.1?SHELF=&DT=20020920180651&CASE=&FS=TRUE> 
and states 'The following statements illustrate the use of an alphanumeric 
intrinsic function and a numeric intrinsic function.'

So... there's two kinds of intrinsic functions, alphanumeric and numeric.  
Some folks have had a bit of experience on what can happen when one 
attempts a numeric manipulation on an alphanumeric field... and on 
<http://publibz.boulder.ibm.com/cgi-bin/bookmgr_OS390/BOOKS/igy3lr10/7.1.2?SHELF=&DT=20020920180651&CASE=&FS=TRUE> 
there's a whole bunch of Function Definitions.

SUM (from the example above) takes only the arguments of I1 or N1 (I 
assume Integer or Numeric)... but LENGTH takes A1, N1, O1, X1 OR U1, ' 
Length of argument in national character positions or in alphanumeric 
character positions or bytes, depending on the argument type.'

My guess is that since LENGTH can take an Alpha it gets a bit upset when 
it is told to SUBTRACT before the operand is evaluated... and it might be 
that SUM goes kerflooie! if one of its operands is alphabetic.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: FUNCTION LENGTH

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T20:40:35-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<zJWdnQrVQ8jp9LXXnZ2dnUVZ_sadnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```

"Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com> wrote in message 
news:4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com...
> Hmm, this surprised me.  I just tried this:
>
…[39 more quoted lines elided]…
>

A numeric function can only be used where an arithmetic expression can be 
specified.  They cannot be used where an integer operand is required.

From the COBOL Language Reference:

Numeric functions A numeric function can be used only where an arithmetic 
expression can be specified. Chapter 22. Intrinsic functions 461

A numeric function can be referenced as an argument for a function that 
allows a numeric argument. A numeric function cannot be used where an 
integer operand is required, even if the particular reference would yield an 
integer value. The INTEGER or INTEGER-PART functions can be used to force 
the type of a numeric argument to be an integer.

 Integer functions An integer function can be used only where an arithmetic 
expression can be specified. An integer function can be referenced as an 
argument for a function that allows an integer argument. Usage notes: v 
identifier-2 of the CALL statement must not be a function-identifier. v The 
COPY statement accepts function-identifiers of all types in the REPLACING 
phrase.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: FUNCTION LENGTH

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2009-06-05T13:59:16-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<aWdWl.275488$641.158137@en-nntp-10.dc1.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<4A279AF8.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <zJWdnQrVQ8jp9LXXnZ2dnUVZ_sadnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
What is posted below is 100% correct for Enterprise COBOL and for the Intrinsic 
Function amendment to the '85 Standard.  In the '02 Standard (and for many other 
compilers) this has been expanded so that a numeric intrinsic function may be 
used where a numeric sending item may be used and similarly for integer 
functions.

If this is a feature that you would like in Enterprise COBOL, then I suggest 
that you submit an "IBM Marketing REQUEST" and reference the SHARE requirement:

  SSLNGC0313595  2002 ISO COBOL - Expand use of Numeric & Integer Functions

currently marked as "RECOGNIZED"
```

## Thread: opencobol language reference — 1 this month / 4 total

_Topics: [`Open-source COBOL (GnuCOBOL, OpenCOBOL, TinyCOBOL)`](topics.md#open-source) · [`Tutorials, books, learning`](topics.md#learning)_

_Thread started [2009-05](2009-05.md#t-a263b97d1f)._

#### ↳ Re: opencobol language reference

- **From:** btiffin@canada.com
- **Date:** 2009-06-04T18:01:24-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<4b8fe27a-0960-4c28-99bb-ec1a26d9f906@r16g2000vbn.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<AEtPl.16554$Ux.2341@tornado.fastwebnet.it>`

```
As Bill pointed out, short answer is no.  But in OC's defence.
OpenCOBOL is open.  There is nothing hidden. Source codes are
available.  It might not always be easy, but nothing in OpenCOBOL is
"impossible" to figure out.  For some major hints on the capabilities,
visit the parser.y source in cobc/ and codegen.c in the same cobc
subdirectory of the source tar ball.

Anyone that wants help with OpenCOBOL, be sure to visit opencobol.org,
add1tocobol.com and the #add1tocobol Freenode IRC channel.  It usually
doesn't take long to get working solutions.

For DISPLAY support, this line

| x_list opt_at_line_column display_upon with_clause on_disp_exception

in the parser and then

opt_at_line_column:
  /* empty */			{ $$ = NULL; }
| _at line_number column_number { $$ = cb_build_pair ($2, $3); }
| _at column_number line_number { $$ = cb_build_pair ($3, $2); }
| _at line_number		{ $$ = cb_build_pair ($2, NULL); }
| _at column_number		{ $$ = cb_build_pair (NULL, $2); }
| AT simple_value		{ $$ = $2; }
;

line_number:
  LINE _number id_or_lit	{ $$ = $3; }
;

column_number:
  COLUMN _number id_or_lit	{ $$ = $3; }
| POSITION _number id_or_lit	{ $$ = $3; }
;

Leads to  DISPLAY variable LINE n POSITION n END-DISPLAY   being a
supported syntax.

This program worked for me here on a Debian GNU/Linux system, with a
OpenCOBOL 1.1 package date of Feb 2009.

      *>
***************************************************************
      *> Purpose:   Try positioned display
      *> Tectonics: cobc -x dispat.cob
      *>
***************************************************************
       identification division.
       program-id. dispat.

       data division.
       working-storage section.
       01 variable pic x(10) value 'abc'.
       01 x        pic 99 value 12.
       01 y        pic 99 value 12.
       01 waiter   pic xx.

      *>
***************************************************************
       procedure division.
       display variable line x position y end-display
       accept waiter at 1010 end-accept

       goback.
       end program dispat.

Oh ... why the waiter?  If not, the screen will flash in and out of
ncurses virtual screen mode too fast to even notice it happen, plus it
shows off some of the alternate syntax available for positioned screen
IO.   ;)

Cheers,
Brian

On May 16, 3:10 am, mlist <ml...@ferramentazizzi.it> wrote:
> Hi,
> there are a language reference for opencobol 1.0/1.1?
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Paride
```

- **Message-ID:** `<4f0a1460-13b5-4278-8713-a7db8ccf000e@x6g2000vbg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<yZgMl.22988$i24.19407@newsfe14.iad>`

```
On the topic at hand,

Beware; I write the OpenCOBOL FAQ, I'm a fan and I'm biased towards
the positive.

OC1.1 passes through 9,000 of the NIST tests ... not sure the line
count, but it hums through them at a fair pace on this old Pentium 4.

Another hint on how it is faring in the real world check out

http://opencobol.add1tocobol.com/#can-opencobol-be-used-for-production-applications

I'll admit, the domain of COBOL usage is not overly ripe with openness
and bragging.  Details are normally proprietary and of a secretive
nature.  Hopefully OpenCOBOL allows the trend to change a little and
COBOL will get some of the internet age lovin' that it deserves.

Cheers,
Brian
```
